1451683405|4.0|Sam Harris can do no wrong, at least so far. Once again he delivers an extremely rational, clear and concise book. This time about one of our most cherished notions, free will.  Harris doesn't waste much time going over other philosophers arguments and although he mentions some scientific experiments (Libbet, W.Grey and more), he doesn't analyze them in depth. What he does is explain why free will can't possibly exist using simple logic.  The truth of the matter is that we are not aware of the neuronal events that precede our mental states. This is indisputable since we cannot account for what emerges into our consciousness. It really is as simple as that. For people who are not familiar with this subject and are not readers of Harris blog this is a great book.<br />And those who are well read in the subject will probably enjoyed it too, I did. My only complaint is that I wish Harris had gotten a little more into the implications of free will being an illusion. He does explain how morality can work but I'm much more interested in how it affects our every day to day lives, as well as our relations with others. But I'm pretty sure that would be a whole book in itself and here Harris seemed to be more interested in simply debunking the nonsensical notion that is free will.<br /><br />Recommended.

B0000041TD|3.0|So many people have praised this as \\"the\\" recording of La Boheme.  I think not.  I listened to this recording years ago - before it ever occurred to me to put a review online - and was disappointed, but I recently listened to it again and decided to stick my finger in the eyes of those who adore it.  First, while I'm not a Pavarotti fan, I have to give credit where it's due; he is the embodiment of youthful ardor, and does a great job.  Second, I am a big Freni fan, and I think she's done another lovely job.  But there the good news ends.  Elizabeth Harwood is just off as Musetta; she sounds, well, old and rough.  Rolando Panerai has some good moments, but it's a very inconsistent performance.  And then there's von Karajan.  His tempos (OK, tempi) and volume are all over the place; sometimes it sounds like it's a chamber piece and sometimes it sounds like a lush and over-orchestrated piece of schmaltz, and sometimes it's slow and sometimes it sounds like von Karajan was late for a bus (or, in his case, a chauffeur-driven Mercedes).  I guess the acid test for me was the second act; La Boheme is a piece of schmaltz, but I still think the brief second act is among the most perfect acts in all opera - and certainly in Italian opera.  In this recording the second act just doesn't work.  Harwood isn't good, von Karajan isn't good.  I'd be inclined to give this recording four stars for Pavarotti and Freni, but I've got to bring it down a peg.<br /><br />One last thought.  I like the Solti/Caballe/Domingo/Milnes/Blegen recording a whole lot ([[ASIN:B000009NIW Puccini: La Boh&egrave;me]]), but I think my desert island recording of Boheme would still be the now ancient one conducted by Sir Thomas Beecham, with Victoria de los Angeles, Jussi Bjoerling and Robert Merrill ([[ASIN:B000063UM0 Puccini: La Boheme (Great Recordings of the Century)]]).  I am not a devotee of old recordings, but that one is a gem.
B0000793ZB|4.0|Recorded at the same time as they did the soundtrack song \\"Don't You Forget About Me\\", \\"Once Upon a Time\\" didn't really reflect the impact of that song but, instead, reflected the musical changes occurring within the band. Although it eschews the glacial feel of their previous albums for a warmer vibe, \\"Once Upon a Time\\" still has quality songs and shows the band searching for a new musical direction while trying to stay as true to their roots as possible.<br /><br />I have mixed feelings about remasters--you never know what you're going to get with the loudness wars, over use of compression and noise reduction that became a hallmark of the late 90's and beyond. While Simon Heyworth's remaster is slightly louder than the original CD and doesn't boast quite the same dynamic range as the original, it also improves on the soundstage (at least compared to the U.S. edition which may have used a different tape source and not the original master tapes), it still sounds pretty good for a modern remaster. Heyworth used much the same techniques that he did with some of the XTC remasters (some of which were very good like [[ASIN:B000T04566 English Settlement]] and some of which were very bad like [[ASIN:B00005ATHO Skylarking]]. In the case of \\"Once Upon a Time\\" and [[ASIN:B0000793ZA Sparkle in the Rain]] Heyworth managed to improve on some of the deficits of the original CD's. That isn't to say this is a perfect remaster--in an ideal world the dynamic range would be kept intact with stronger detail, depth and soundstage as a result. Nevertheless, \\"Once\\" and \\"Sparkle\\" both sound quite good.<br /><br />Unfortunately, there aren't ANY bonus tracks. This would have been an ideal place to add the b-sides, remixes and even \\"(Don't You)Forget About Me\\" since it was recorded around the same time. The artwork is a pathetic reproduction of the original graphics for the album--with lyrics too small to read and even album credits that are in the tiniest fond imaginable. Virgin could have used a bit more imagination when it came to the packaging here and the content.<br /><br />Will you like this remaster? If you want the full dynamic range of the original CD maybe not but if you don't mind some of the loss of that range trading it off for the use of better tapes, detail and soundstage, then this might be the disc for you.used a bit more imagination when it came to the packaging here and the content.<br /><br />Will you like this remaster? But Katniss' personal journey from naive to war weary is heart-wrenching and emotionally exhausting.<br /><br />The characterization is better than I've seen since McMaster-Bujold's early Vorkosigan books. If you read fiction to really care about the characters, this book is incredible. The interrelationships between the three leading characters are downright captivating. You follow these characters through their emotional arcs, and by the end of these books their crises are the reader's--and that's about all a reader can ask.<br /><br />So much for my impression as a reader. As an aspiring author myself, I've only got two things to say:<br /><br />1) I wish I had written it, and<br />2) I hope mine are as well-received.<br /><br />Collins has given every writer out there an example of what characterization and emotional development should be. Masterful!

1451648537|4.0|I am not sure that anyone from my generation is a proper person to review or really appreciate a biography of Steve Jobs. Mine was the generation where mother made toast in the oven; many a telephone call was made on a phone on which you called \\"Central\\" and were connected; news came either from the newspaper delivered to the door morning and evening or from the Saturday Evening Post or maybe Colliers Magazine, nothing more; and I can't remember anyone who had a radio.<br /><br /> Over the years, of course, that has changed and those of us who are left from the generation to which I refer have become accustomed to most but certainly not all of the gadgets of modern technology. Yes, some can use computers and maybe even a cell phone, but the iPod, the iPhone and the iPad are still in the iCloud for most of us; and those are the instruments that Jobs essentially created and gave to us. If we use them at all we don't use them with the effortless ease, the spontaneity, or the cheerful aplomb of our children, grandchildren and great grandchildren. So I have read this biography with a certain caution, not sure that I have fully appreciated the immense changes the man has brought to our society, not sure, for instance, that I completely understand the significance between an open and closed platform or grasp the elegance of the design of the iPad, and not sure that I can fairly evaluate the man and his work.<br /><br /> One thing I am sure of, however, is that the black nature of the man makes a fair judgment of his work difficult and that, important as he was, there were others too  - mentioned but not honored in the book - who were equally important , equally talented and equally entitled to share the technology stage with him.<br /><br /> This being said, however, I have no problem with appreciating good writing and the exercise of superlative literary and personal judgment on the part the author of this biography, a book which presents an accurate word picture, warts and all, of a major figure, an innovator, a mover and shaker of twenty first century technology, someone who \\"knew what the public wanted before it knew what it wanted\\". Even before he began this project, Walter Isaacson had been a solid biographer of Benjamin Franklin, Henry Kissinger, Albert Einstein and others, working mostly if not completely from secondary sources. Here, however, in his Jobs biography he has worked primarily from original sources. He talked to Jobs - more than forty interviews or conversations. He talked to his former colleagues, his friends and his enemies, the Jobs family near and far, his business associates. In short as a skilled reporter, a former editor of Time and President of CNN he was no lightweight interviewer. Nor, because of his journalistic experience was he any lightweight when it came to putting it all together in an intelligent, readable and accurate story of a man who was his own worst enemy and who has helped define our present by his genius.<br /><br /> Speaking of genius: When the seeds of genius are sprinkled among the human race at birth one hopes that the human vessel which receives them is normal - think Leonardo Da Vinci, J.S. Bach. Wm. Shakespeare, among others - but sometimes the vessel is flawed; and the vessel that was Steve Jobs was flawed beyond any conventional ability to forgive. He was eccentric and narcissistic to the line beyond which lies madness. He was unforgiving, cruel, selfish, unsympathetic, and disloyal to friends and associates. Only to his wife and children, to his adoptive parents and his sister Mona did he exhibit any sign of love or affection. He had no sense of humor, no humility, no gentility and no love for his fellow man or for his life or for that of others. In short, as a person he was simply terrible. And I doubt he ever experienced happiness - even for a moment. Triumph? Maybe. But Solace? Love? Tenderness? Peace? Never! I cannot imagine a sadder life. Yet his genius, his insights, his instincts and his uncontrollable work ethic gave us the best tools - the Mac, the iPod, the iPhone and the iPad - with which to navigate and to find our way in the huge fathomless sea of data and information , the sea of music, art, literature and experience which lies by our bedside and at our doorstep..<br /><br /> So when you read these 600 pages  - and I hope you do - forget the warts on the man, enjoy the story and the way it has been told by a brilliant writer and be aware that this is a once in a lifetime opportunity to read the life story of one of the most brilliant, beguiling, eccentric and important innovators of our time written by one of our very best biographers.

0061743526|5.0|I know more than one person who considers To Kill a Mockingbird their favorite book. My friend Bob cherishes his first edition and speaks glowingly of the time when the author signed his copy. Ms. Lee, who grew up in Monroeville, Alabama, was a young child during the famous Scottsboro, Alabama Case (nine black men were accused of raping two white women). This apparently had a significant influence on her as she wrote the book in the late 1950's. It was published at the beginning of the Civil Rights Movement, won the Pulitzer Prize in 1961, and two years later was made into an Academy Award winning film with Gregory Peck as Atticus Finch.<br /><br />It's an endearing story told through the eyes of a nine year old girl, Scout, and it meant several things to me:<br /><br />It affirms that goodness ultimately overcomes evil, but always at a cost.<br />It condemns racial prejudices--as well as the fear and prejudices directed toward the Boo Radley's of the world (who in the end becomes the protector and a symbol of goodness).<br />Innocents can and do suffer from man's stupidity, e.g. Tom Robinson. The title is really about innocence, as Miss Maudie explains: \\" Mockingbird's don't do one thing but sing their hearts out for us. That's why it's a sin to kill a mockingbird.\\"<br />It reinforced a personal belief--that people are not basically good--a worldview leading to the assumption that everything bad a person does, is society's fault. I believe that people are born innocent and we grow up absorbing, learning, and understanding that good and evil are a part of us, if we ever grow up to maturity, that is. What Atticus Finch teaches his children is to understand that evil is a part of the world and we should treat people with empathy, as though we were \\"walking in their shoes.\\" His understanding and treatment of crass old Mrs. Dubose was a lesson in patient understanding--her life was mixed with great courage yet she was openly racist, which Atticus abhorred. Like most, she was a person blended with both good and bad qualities of personality.<br />I found it interesting that, in the end, the protection afforded by Boo Radley was built on a lie of what happened on that dark path from the school pageant to home. It raises unanswered questions....<br />I read in an old magazine that Harper Lee lives half her time in New York and half in Alabama. She is no recluse--she can be seen in shops and restaurants--but she rarely gives interviews and most people wouldn't recognize her if they saw her. She has always lived a normal and comfortable life, not the life of a celebrity author. To Kill a Mockingbird is her only published book and one gets the sense that she doesn't really care--she said everything she had to say about life in her one great book. Your cultural barometer doesn't have to go anywhere east for you to recognize this as a tense, horrifying thriller.  Beyond that, it does a great job of expressing the awkward, emotional strain of 9th graders, faced suddenly with certain death.  Their decisions--who to kiss, who to kill--shape them in our eyes.<br /><br />Of course, the stand out performance here is that of Takeshi Kitano.  His twitchy, bizarre delivery haunts you, but also leaves room for sympathy, which is no small feat.  Sympathizing with a man who murders children is tough, even for those of us who have uncles just like that.<br /><br />For return viewers, my recommendation is to purchase this edition.  Largely, for the translation.  While decent in earlier editions, by the end of the film (particularly the final three requiems), the translation becomes unbearably indecipherable.  This edition redelivers those lines poignantly and perfectly.<br /><br />So it's worth the money, nerds.  Slap that cash down.

0439023513|2.0|The book deserves a 2.4/5, so I rounded down. I really liked the first 2 books, but this one left me feeling disappointed at first. Now after I've had time to think, I feel mostly manipulated. While all books manipulate you, the best ones don't make it obvious like this one. One of my biggest gripes is that Gale never served any purpose in the series besides being part of a love triangle. Some people claim this series is better than Twilight, but that series handled the love problem much better than HG. Also, I just didn't like Katniss in this one. She never appeared strong, assured, or even decisive. Few, if any, of the people acted realistically.<br /><br />The author wants to portray the horrors of war, but does it in the most contrived manner to elicit a response from the reader. Things happen and people react in ways that don't make sense. We don't even know if the government has gone through any true change. The people change in ways that don't make sense, as everyone around Katniss who doesn't die pretty much abandon her in other ways. It all seems calculated to make us feel sad about war, but really it's just the author manipulating us. And many of us don't like it.<br /><br />***spoilers*****spoilers****spoilers****<br /><br />Prim's death was created only to manipulate the audience. It served almost no purpose other than to show you can't trust Coin, but there were other and better ways to show that. Next, everyone abandons Katniss at the end when she's at her lowest. Neither Gale, nor her mother can even bother to visit her for no valid reason. If I only had 1 family member left who had been through severe trauma, I would not leave her alone. Finnick's death was also pointless. He should never have been in the military in the first place. He had his loved one safe, he was married, and he easily could've been used as a symbol of the hope a new world can come with a new gov't. His death was to manipulate the audience.<br /><br />Katniss's strength was undermined in this book. Not just because she spends all her time wondering if she should help the rebels in the beginning, but at the end when she loses herself. People who come home from war unable to function in life are rare. People die all the time; loved ones die every day. The fact that Katniss cannot deal with Prim's death, nor could Katniss's mother, shows incredible mental instability below the average person. We've never dealt with the hardship described in Hunger Games which tends to make people immune to loss, but we all manage to continue to live life despite the death of friends and family. We make more, we move on. This is hinted at in the end, but it's overshadowed and almost lost. Just do your family history before the 1900s, little children died every day from accidents, weather, or lack of medicine. People who are used to it, while they aren't immune, don't break down worse than people today.<br /><br />Other reviews have described it better than I have, but this book leaves you almost empty. Yes, war is hell. But there's always hope and there are people ready to do the noble and selfless thing that needs to be done. Katniss was never selfless, everything she did was for her own benefit. Maybe it appeared selfless because she was willing to die, but it was always because she didn't want to suffer with the guilt of letting her loved ones die or losing her loved ones. She didn't have to be perfect, but she should have been stronger at the end.<br /><br />Some think the ending was brave because it reflected reality better than many stories. Yet, we are left without any knowledge about what is going on. We are just assuming the new gov't is perfect and treats everyone fairly and that's it, despite the distrust built up against all gov't people throughout the entire book. It undermines itself. This book leaves you feeling hollow and somewhat empty inside, with a tiny glimmer of hope.

B00325NM6E|5.0|This is for the hardcover edition of Absolute Watchmen.<br /><br />If you've read the story, you know what it's about. If you've never read it... what are you waiting for, an invitation?<br /><br />To business. The Absolute format reprints comic books (yes, COMICS; they're not \\"graphic novels\\") at a noticeably larger size and, in cases like Watchmen, restored color. In relation to that, Absolute Watchmen looks AMAZING! The size, the color, the high quality paper, it's definitely flawless in terms of presentation.<br /><br />As far as extras, you get sketches, snippets of Moore's pitch, subsequent revisions and even a sample of his heavily detailed scripts. There are also essays from Moore and Gibbons written not long after Watchmen was completed.<br /><br />The binding is strong and sturdy enough to last for years. The book is heavy enough to lay flat and open to whichever page you want without need for a paper weight. The book also includes a ribbon bookmark.<br /><br />Nothing, NOTHING, about this edition was done on the cheap. DC spared no expense. They seriously knocked themselves out to make this the best possible presentation, and it shows.<br /><br />Skip those ridiculous prequels and stick with the original. You'll be glad you did.

1451627289|4.0|As I first time Stephen King reader I was far from disappointed with this book. It was well researched and well written. The one problem I saw in it was that of excessive explicit language. The book begins in 2011 and the language was appropriate for the time but as Jake travels back to the 1960's, easing up on the swearing would have added to the stories dialect. At a time when nearly everything we think of today as \\"normal\\" was very controversial I didn't find it very convincing to have young woman repeatedly using foul language in day to day life and in public. Aside from that this book is wonderful, a real page-turner. While many time travel books seem to be a scar on even the greatest authors career this one was certainly a win.
